  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_8d42535b-3cc5-4f8e-9457-998ab7db3ac6'
      });

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer4248755420909214161">The award-winning poet Anne Carson reinvents a genre in <i>Autobiography of Red</i>, a stunning work that is both a novel and a poem, both an unconventional re-creation of an ancient Greek myth and a wholly original coming-of-age story set in the present.<br/><br/>Geryon, a young boy who is also a winged red monster, reveals the volcanic terrain of his fragile, tormented soul in an autobio</span>
  <span id="freeText4248755420909214161" style="display:none">The award-winning poet Anne Carson reinvents a genre in <i>Autobiography of Red</i>, a stunning work that is both a novel and a poem, both an unconventional re-creation of an ancient Greek myth and a wholly original coming-of-age story set in the present.<br /><br />Geryon, a young boy who is also a winged red monster, reveals the volcanic terrain of his fragile, tormented soul in an autobiography he begins at the age of five. As he grows older, Geryon escapes his abusive brother and affectionate but ineffectual mother, finding solace behind the lens of his camera and in the arms of a young man named Herakles, a cavalier drifter who leaves him at the peak of infatuation. When Herakles reappears years later, Geryon confronts again the pain of his desire and embarks on a journey that will unleash his creative imagination to its fullest extent. By turns whimsical and haunting, erudite and accessible, richly layered and deceptively simple, <i>Autobiography of Red</i> is a profoundly moving portrait of an artist coming to terms with the fantastic accident of who he is.<br /><br />"A profound love story . . . sensuous and funny, poignant, musical and tender." -- <i>The New York Times Book Review</i><br /><br />"A deeply odd and immensely engaging book. . . . [Carson] exposes with passionate force the mythic underlying the explosive everyday."  -- <i>The Village Voice</i><br /><br />A <i>NEW YORK TIMES</i> NOTABLE BOOK OF THE YEAR<br /><br />National book Critics Circle Award Finalist</span>
  <a data-text-id="4248755420909214161" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>
</div>

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/4484305?book_show_action=true">Aug 13, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/275400-laurie-neighbors?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer4484305" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13506947565928673458">Oh, you should read this book. It's smart and sweet and tender and original. It's erotic, but just under your skin. It's a novel in verse, but don't let that deter you. You can pick it up off the shelf and settle into a big armchair in the bookstore and read the whole thing for free in an hour (even relishing and re-reading parts). But then you'll probably want to buy it anyway so that you can take it home and sleep with it under your pillow for the rest of your life.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2631315294" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2631315294" />
    <a title="Kenny" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1642452-kenny"><img alt="Kenny" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1472073986p2/1642452.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2631315294?book_show_action=true">Sep 14, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kenny" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kenny" href="/user/show/1642452-kenny">Kenny</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1642452-kenny?shelf=women-poets">women-poets</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1642452-kenny?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1642452-kenny?shelf=desert-island-books">desert-island-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1642452-kenny?shelf=queer-lit">queer-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1642452-kenny?shelf=queer-poets">queer-poets</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2631315294" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14821026022056508802"><b><i>“Sometimes a journey makes itself necessary.”</i><br/>Anne Carson ~~ Autobiography of Red</b><br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1568541182i/28151185.png' alt='1' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>Anne Carson’s <b>Autobiography Of Red</b> took my breath away. I don't think I've felt this way about a book since I first discovered Virginia Woolf. I was stunned by Carson's poetic novel.<br/><br/><b>Autobiography Of Red</b> is packed with so many emotions, perhaps the most glaring is Geryon ~~ the protagonist’s ~~ continual struggle with loneliness and loss ~~ something I can relate to all to well. From an early age, Geryon must deal wit</span>
  <span id="freeText14821026022056508802" style="display:none"><b><i>“Sometimes a journey makes itself necessary.”</i><br>Anne Carson ~~ Autobiography of Red</b><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1568541182i/28151185.png" alt="1" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Anne Carson’s <b>Autobiography Of Red</b> took my breath away. I don't think I've felt this way about a book since I first discovered Virginia Woolf. I was stunned by Carson's poetic novel.<br><br><b>Autobiography Of Red</b> is packed with so many emotions, perhaps the most glaring is Geryon ~~ the protagonist’s ~~ continual struggle with loneliness and loss ~~ something I can relate to all to well. From an early age, Geryon must deal with a great loss that would devastate any child ~~ the loss of a parent. While Carson doesn’t provide much insight, it’s implied that Geryon’s mother and father are not together. “<i>Every second Tuesday in winter Geryon’s father and brother went to hockey practice. / Geryon and his mother had supper alone</i>”. Not only is Geryon without a father figure in the household, but also, it seems that Geryon does not see his father at all, as the brother does. This would be difficult for any child to cope with.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1568541182i/28151186.png" alt="1" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>This creates a feeling of loss and loneliness that permeates the entire house. Geryon and his mother eat supper alone; while their mother works, Geryon and his brother are babysat. <b>Autobiography Of Red</b> is full of moments when Geryon completely experiences loneliness. In a loss of innocence and trust, his brother molests him ~~ and there is nothing he can do nothing about it ~~ he’s afraid to go to his mother, and his father’s nowhere to be see – this leaves Geryon feeling even more isolated and alone. When Geryon and his mother are alone in the house, they turn on all the lights in every room ~~ even in the rooms they have no intention of being in. Why? Well, it’s a defense against desolation. Turning on all the lights makes them feel less alone. Fear is equated with darkness in this world. In this world even the empty fruit bowl is equated with loneliness.<br><br>But, Geryon’s journey is also a journey of self-awareness, discovery, and acceptance. The entire novel concerns itself with the battle between inside versus outside ~~ physically, spiritually, sexually, literally, and metaphorically. Geryon’s early life is built around his inner world rather than the world around him. He is the opposite of most children.<br><br>Geryon’s world changes drastically when he’s becomes a love struck adolescent. Like all of us, puberty alters Geryon’s outlook in magical ways. After befriending, and falling in love with Herakles, Geryon begins to live outside of himself ~~ even the scenes shift from inside to outside. When Geryon visits Herakles’ house they spend their time outside on the lawn or the back porch. Together they take road trips together. As Geryon becomes more involved with Herakles, he inhabits the outside world. Our hero is no longer lonely. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1568541182i/28151187._SY540_.jpg" alt="1" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Subsequently, when Geryon and Herakles separate, we initially see Geryon slide backwards into his old self. He returns home to his mother. Their home is now filled with tension. With Herakles no longer in the picture, Geryon experiences a crisis like none he’s ever known before. He now needs to know who he is. This is a far cry from the inner world Geryon experienced as a boy ~~ before he came to begin accepting himself. Now that he’s aware of his desires, and sexual orientation, he can no longer hide himself from the world.<br><br>Years later Geryon comes across Herakles on a trip to Argentina. Herakles' new lover Ancash forms the third point of a love triangle. The novel ends, ambiguously, with Geryon, Ancash, and Herakles stopping outside a ... <br><br>At last, Geryon is transformed. He’s less self-loathing, less depressed, not fully satisfied, but having experienced and learned so much; from philosophers and photography and lovers, from receiving whooping from Herakles’ current lover, Ancash, to accepting himself for who and what he is, he finally chooses to live.<br><br>In the end, I have to agree with Alice Munro. "<i>This book is amazing–I haven’t discovered any writing in years so marvelously disturbing.</i>"<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1568541181i/28151184._SY540_.jpg" alt="1" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>
  <a data-text-id="14821026022056508802" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_478478279" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/478478279" />
    <a title="Mariel" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/647547-mariel"><img alt="Mariel" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1451321467p2/647547.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/478478279?book_show_action=true">Dec 15, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mariel" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mariel" href="/user/show/647547-mariel">Mariel</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">gaiety transfiguring all that dread</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Mariel by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/237469-mir">Mir</a>
  </div>


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer478478279" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16438932589321099460"><blockquote> What do they think about? Floating in there. All night.<br/>Nothing.<br/>That's impossible.<br/>Why?<br/>You can't be alive and think about nothing. You can't but you're not a whale.<br/>Why would it be different?<br/>Why should it be the same? But I look in their eyes and I see them thinking.<br/>Nonsense. It is yourself you see- it's guilt.<br/>Guilt? Why would I be guilty about whales? Not my fault they're in a tank.<br/>Exactly. So why are you guilty- whose tank are you in?</blockquote><br/><br/>Australian goodreader Sean (account since nuked, sadly) wrote </span>
  <span id="freeText16438932589321099460" style="display:none"><blockquote> What do they think about? Floating in there. All night.<br />Nothing.<br />That's impossible.<br />Why?<br />You can't be alive and think about nothing. You can't but you're not a whale.<br />Why would it be different?<br />Why should it be the same? But I look in their eyes and I see them thinking.<br />Nonsense. It is yourself you see- it's guilt.<br />Guilt? Why would I be guilty about whales? Not my fault they're in a tank.<br />Exactly. So why are you guilty- whose tank are you in?</blockquote><br /><br />Australian goodreader Sean (account since nuked, sadly) wrote a review of Garden, Ashes (a wonderful book) on Christmas. He said it was his "Christmas miracle", saving the day. That had made me smile because I had felt that way about Anne Carson's Autobiography of Red. It may seem a bit weird to write that now because days after it have needed "saving". Some kind of weight on the empty place. It doesn't matter. It is enough to have it when I can have it. In the end he gets to fly, to swim. I just breathed. If you have to know how to look at it then I can look at it refracted through them. Just don't stop doing that.<br /><br /><blockquote> We are amazing beings,<br />Geryon is thinking. We are neighbors of fire.<br />And now time is rushing towards them<br />where they stand side by side with arms touching, immortality on their faces, night at their back.</blockquote><br /><br />Geryon is a little boy and he goes to his mother. He stays by her side. They are up all night. They make dinner. They turn on every light in the house. Geryon saves the voices his mother makes when she reads to him. He keeps them safe by allowing the babysitter to read in voices she cannot get wrong, because they are not his mother's voices. She comes home to him when he is awake. He must have forgotten to stay awake for her. Would it ever be warmer than this. Days turn into nights and secrets. He meets him. Herakles on his red wing tips carrying dust. Folding back arms for a hug. She still folds his t-shirts. Mother can hug him no more. I wanted him to stay in that kitchen with his mother but he couldn't. I wonder if she turns on lights without him, or just watches the empty fruit bowl. Still life painting without the life subject. Geryon always cries when they make love. Herakles couldn't wait forever. He's so impatient I wonder if he's just waiting to get old. When he's old he'll be impatient to be young. Herakles hides on his grandmother's porch. I imagine he uses his grandmother as something of his own when he wants to shut out. He would use her as something of his own, like a secret, when he wants to invite in. This is where I live, my family, my end of the sofa. Geryon floats. Suspenders under his coat, wings kept in, he flies above the land to where the Nazis and Argentinians live. He studies volcanoes. Mountains with left over ashes and fires to be started kept within. Herakles comes back. Herakles has Ancash. Paintings that look like something from far away. When you get up close he is back in your bed. I wouldn't want to be in love with Herakles. I would be afraid to dance like Angela on My So-Called Life (to "Tears of the Sun") when she believes she's finally over Jordan Catalano. It's a dance to convince oneself. It's a denial and the only thing you can do. Don't want to find out if you're wrong. Geryon is close to the mountain. He could jump in, if he doesn't learn another place. He could have made a sacrifice. Autobiography of Red was my Christmas miracle when Geryon is okay without Herakles. I really needed that. No one can kill your red dog or anything if you can be okay with yourself. It's something about not wanting anyone else's secret. There is no power there anymore. Something about not living in dreams. Or maybe it was his new boyfriend sitting up in the cold outside. Would you look into the beluga whales and see only yourself in the tank or just wish they could swim and keep on. I'd put something with wings on top of that Christmas tree and light all the candles for that. </span>
  <a data-text-id="16438932589321099460" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1631450736?book_show_action=true">Apr 13, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32804270-michael?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1631450736" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14664759575029014846">An arresting novel-in-verse about art, desire, and abuse, <i>Autobiography of Red</i> blurs the line between the mythic and the mundane. The work charts the brief life of a marginal figure from classical myth: Geryon, the red-winged monster slain by Hercules as part of his tenth labour. In plain verse, Carson invents a modern, tragic backstory for Geryon, framing him as an abused child who, as an adult, becomes a sensitive photographer and the much-wronged lover of Hercules. Across dozens of fast-movin</span>
  <span id="freeText14664759575029014846" style="display:none">An arresting novel-in-verse about art, desire, and abuse, <i>Autobiography of Red</i> blurs the line between the mythic and the mundane. The work charts the brief life of a marginal figure from classical myth: Geryon, the red-winged monster slain by Hercules as part of his tenth labour. In plain verse, Carson invents a modern, tragic backstory for Geryon, framing him as an abused child who, as an adult, becomes a sensitive photographer and the much-wronged lover of Hercules. Across dozens of fast-moving chapters, the author considers the redemptive powers of art and questions what it means for two men to love each other. The project’s more interesting than affecting; much of the imagery is understated, bordering on dull, and the storyline’s not that intellectually stimulating. While this is her most famous work, Carson’s written much better.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14664759575029014846" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

Geryon had always<br /> had this trouble: a word like each,<br /> when he stared at it, would disassemble itself into separate letters and go.<br /> A space for its meaning remained there but blank.<br /> The letters themselves could be found hung on branches or furniture in the area.<br /> What does each mean?<br /><br />Q:<br />VI. MEANWHILE IN HEAVEN<br /> Athena was looking down through the floor<br /> Of the glass-bottomed boat Athena pointed<br /> Zeus looked Him (c)<br />Q:<br />Q:<br />He loved lightning He lived on an island His mother was a<br /> Nymph of a river that ran to the sea His father was a gold<br /> Cutting tool Old scholia say that Stesichoros says that<br /> Geryon had six hands and six feet and wings He was red and<br /> His strange red cattle excited envy Herakles came and<br /> Killed him for his cattle<br /> <br /> The dog too (c)<br />Q:<br />They watched each other,<br /> this odd pair. He is looking at the men.<br /> I mean the fire, says Ancash.<br /> Herakles grins in the dark. Ancash watches the flames.<br /> We are amazing beings,<br /> Geryon is thinking. We are neighbors of fire.<br /> And now time is rushing towards them<br /> where they stand side by side with arms touching, immortality on their faces,<br /> night at their back. (c)</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer17738447333751458275">I read this book when I was about twenty, and it was the greatest thing that I'd ever read. Then I purposely avoided rereading it for over a decade after that, terrified that it couldn't be as incredible as I remembered.<br/><br/>So recently I did have to reread it, for school. And no, it didn't do to me what it did the first time that I read it, and it no longer seems light years greater than any other book in human history. HOWEVER, it is still really great and one of my all-time favorites, and I'd reco</span>
  <span id="freeText17738447333751458275" style="display:none">I read this book when I was about twenty, and it was the greatest thing that I'd ever read. Then I purposely avoided rereading it for over a decade after that, terrified that it couldn't be as incredible as I remembered.<br /><br />So recently I did have to reread it, for school. And no, it didn't do to me what it did the first time that I read it, and it no longer seems light years greater than any other book in human history. HOWEVER, it is still really great and one of my all-time favorites, and I'd recommend it to pretty much anyone, especially if you have any feeling at all in your heart for classicism. I wish it weren't cliche to say that Anne Carson is a goddess. Anne Carson is a goddess, and were I ever to build a temple or sacrifice mammals it would be to her.</span>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1679177704" />

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1679177704?book_show_action=true">Jul 18, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Thomas" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Thomas" href="/user/show/2018505-thomas">Thomas</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2018505-thomas?shelf=lgbtq">lgbtq</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2018505-thomas?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2018505-thomas?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1679177704" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15081419946476418426">One of those books that everyone else loves and I just did not get, oops. <i>Autobiography of Red</i> has so many things I find relatable and should have liked more: a main character who experienced abuse in his childhood, a queer love story, and a plot that features the importance of art. Unfortunately this novel in verse did not resonate with me. I wanted more character development and a more substantial plot. Perhaps I do not "get" poetry or I have been spoiled by my favorite gay novels, including <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1362663464?book_show_action=false&from_review_page=1' rel='nofollow noopener'>
</a></span>
  <span id="freeText15081419946476418426" style="display:none">One of those books that everyone else loves and I just did not get, oops. <i>Autobiography of Red</i> has so many things I find relatable and should have liked more: a main character who experienced abuse in his childhood, a queer love story, and a plot that features the importance of art. Unfortunately this novel in verse did not resonate with me. I wanted more character development and a more substantial plot. Perhaps I do not "get" poetry or I have been spoiled by my favorite gay novels, including <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1362663464?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <i>A Little Life</i>
</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/494453741?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <i>Aristotle and Dante Discover the Secrets of the Universe</i>
</a>. Even though I did not love this one I am glad others did.</span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/542017444?book_show_action=true">Feb 20, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Aubrey" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Aubrey" href="/user/show/4119460-aubrey">Aubrey</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780375401336" href="/book/show/1406018.Autobiography_of_Red">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer542017444" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9894226176148034033"><blockquote>
  <i>It was taking him a very long while<br/>to set up the camera. Enormous pools of a moment kept opening around his hands<br/>each time he tried to move them.</i>
</blockquote>Every so often my education comes in handy when I am confronted by a piece that does not seize me by the heart and wring it till I weep like it has apparently done for most everyone else. One could say peer pressure, or one could admit to capitalism and how a measure of discipline is needed in analyzing any work that is mandated, regardless of perso</span>
  <span id="freeText9894226176148034033" style="display:none"><blockquote>
  <i>It was taking him a very long while<br />to set up the camera. Enormous pools of a moment kept opening around his hands<br />each time he tried to move them.</i>
</blockquote>Every so often my education comes in handy when I am confronted by a piece that does not seize me by the heart and wring it till I weep like it has apparently done for most everyone else. One could say peer pressure, or one could admit to capitalism and how a measure of discipline is needed in analyzing any work that is mandated, regardless of personal adoration. Besides, this is one of those works that I really need to go back and reread <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1371.The_Iliad" title="The Iliad by Homer" rel="nofollow noopener">The Iliad</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1381.The_Odyssey" title="The Odyssey by Homer" rel="nofollow noopener">The Odyssey</a> and co. for, albeit I dislike necessitating the swallowing of the same old canon for a work that should stand on its own, albeit there's many a confabulation of myth and written and blasphemy tickling my brain that I would so like to pin down to the count, albeit what the hell am I doing in the major of English if I want to forgo more reading for reading's sake, so. Sometimes the internal to-and-fro works like these provoke is worth the cost of purely academic parsing alone.<br /><br />What have you written that could strike you blind? Not lie or truth but sacrilege, much like that fine line between fanfiction and kindred souls of Pulitzer Prize winners that so troubled a professor when I responded to his offer of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11899.The_Hours" title="The Hours by Michael Cunningham" rel="nofollow noopener">The Hours</a> with lack of interest. I'd believe there was a difference in type rather than quality were the halls not so full of Shakespeare and Milton and those who knew what one should steal in order to enjoy the better legacy of Prometheus. Go big, go ancient, go poetic and/or historical, go foundering on the threat of military might or the wracking of the very own soul, the mind of a male of red-rued means that bypasses death for the photo volcano and finds a refuge, like so many of us, in art. Perhaps not from love, or wings, or child on child sexual assault, but from real to representation nonetheless. There's reasons why Plato banished the stuff.<br /><br />I didn't like this as much as <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/150252.The_Beauty_of_the_Husband_A_Fictional_Essay_in_29_Tangos" title="The Beauty of the Husband A Fictional Essay in 29 Tangos by Anne Carson" rel="nofollow noopener">The Beauty of the Husband: A Fictional Essay in 29 Tangos</a>. Course, I was reading poetry a lot more regularly back then, so I may be thinking myself set on track while in reality dangling preciously off the back of the horse. Still, I can appreciate the feeling of the need to pay tribute in whys and wherefores that take wordselves far more seriously than the world deems fit to grant. Imagine all those objects rising up against their subjects and tearing eyes from their sockets. What a riot.<blockquote>
  <i>People, thought Geryon,<br />for whom life<br />is a marvelous adventure. He moved off into the tragicomedy of the crowd.</i>
</blockquote></span>
  <a data-text-id="9894226176148034033" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_1394381997" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1394381997" />
    <a title="Neal Adolph" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5459072-neal-adolph"><img alt="Neal Adolph" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1313534109p2/5459072.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1394381997?book_show_action=true">Sep 17, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Neal Adolph" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Neal Adolph" href="/user/show/5459072-neal-adolph">Neal Adolph</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5459072-neal-adolph?shelf=to-reread">to-reread</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5459072-neal-adolph?shelf=novellas">novellas</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5459072-neal-adolph?shelf=north-american">north-american</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5459072-neal-adolph?shelf=2015-reads">2015-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5459072-neal-adolph?shelf=woman-author">woman-author</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5459072-neal-adolph?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1394381997" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16122740221826512136">In my short life I have learned that short novels need to be read for as long as they can be. Then the good ones should be reread. This is a challenge. These short novels masquerade themselves as something that you can appreciate in a few hours of reading. Yes, you can read The Lover in an afternoon, or The Hour of the Star in a few hours on a park bench with a good coffee in hand, or you can sit with The Crying of Lot 49 one Tuesday evening and get through it all. If you have the ability to han</span>
  <span id="freeText16122740221826512136" style="display:none">In my short life I have learned that short novels need to be read for as long as they can be. Then the good ones should be reread. This is a challenge. These short novels masquerade themselves as something that you can appreciate in a few hours of reading. Yes, you can read The Lover in an afternoon, or The Hour of the Star in a few hours on a park bench with a good coffee in hand, or you can sit with The Crying of Lot 49 one Tuesday evening and get through it all. If you have the ability to handle the cruelty, almost anything by Coetzee can be easily consumed in a single sitting with Glenn Gould's two recordings of the Goldberg Variations playing in the background. Yes, you can even enjoy them in that time. This is true. But I must give myself some space to contradict myself here. None of these books can be read in a few hours. Short novels are the hardest novels to write, they need space to breathe between rapturous reading sessions. They deserve time to become themselves in the same way that larger novels do. The good ones count among some of the best works of written words - give them life by giving them more time than they need. <br /><br />I haven't done this yet with Anne Carson's Autobiography of Red, though I did give it more than a week to grow and become something that lived with me for a while. That's a long time for a book that is only 150 pages long, and in poetry-ish form, and so those pages aren't all that full. I'll read it again though. This is a beautiful book, short story, poem, that deserves that extra time and extra effort. And it will reveal itself all the more.<br /><br />Some reviews on GoodReads have gone to the trouble of capturing a perfect excerpt of writing from Red's story. I haven't done that - I've never done that for any of my reviews, though I have written some of these lines in a notebook I've reserved for wonderful writing from books which range from good to bad. Go read some of these excerpts. They won't make sense to you now. But come back after having finished the story, book, poem, and you will recall that moment perfectly. It will likely have a stronger impact than it did when you first read it, or when you read through the poem chapter a second time to make sense of whatever emotion it was that you felt in that flicker. Something about this prose drills into your brain. <br /><br />Which is interesting in the best way possible.<br /><br />This is a simple coming-of-age story, where the life of the protagonist, Geryon, is placed in relation with that of Herakles. If you know your ancient Greek poetry these names might mean something to you. They didn't to me. Well, Herakles did, but only because of childhood stories and Disney movies and painted Greek pottery and silly adult erotic fantasies I've acquired about perfect hulking male bodies. Regardless, this simple coming-of-age story, taking for its inspiration ancient Greek epic poems, is as far as possible from being either Ancient or Greek or simple as can be imagined. <br /><br />It is set in the modern day, in South America and America-ish. It is set in both the real and unreal world, and the line between the two is ubiquitous and invisible.<br /><br />It is highly original in form and shape and approach.<br /><br />It is beautifully written. Shockingly well-written. Remarkably well-written.<br /><br />It is heart-breaking and honest and at times its vulnerability is nothing short of disruptive.<br /><br />It has profound things to say about relationships and adequacy.<br /><br />It is humanity and human and monstrous. All of this is important in this poem, short story, novel, but you must figure out why on your own.<br /><br />I don't cry why I read, because I'm a man and I think that means I can only cry in shower or something like that. And so saying that this book made me cry would be a lie and I don't like to misrepresent either myself or the ways in which twenty-first century masculinity in Saskatchewan has profoundly damaged my ability to be my natural self, but there is a moment in this book that has left me profoundly sad. I related to it far too well. It is captured in one word - which those who have read the poem, short story, novel will recall.<br /><br /><i>Degrading.</i><br /><br />Good lord that word has so much weight. What a monster this book has unleashed! <br /><br />And the most horrible thing is that the intimacy which makes that word so ugly in this book is a result of those very same silly adult erotic fantasies I've acquired about perfect hulking male bodies. Monstrosity turns me on. Dammit.<br /><br />The Autobiography of Red is worth a week's worth of reading, deserves a spot on your shelf, and is more than worthy of the second reading that it will certainly end up getting after you set it down and let it breathe and unsettle you. If this isn't a book that is taking up some space in your home, I suggest you go out and pick it up. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16122740221826512136" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText5682984204890652177" style="display:none">I really loved the translated section of this—I think Anne Carson has a way of conveying thoughts and feelings that is impressive to say the least. I do not know if I can say the rest of this resonated with me. <br /><br />I began very much loving this, but the second half meanders, never really coming to any kind of release for me—and I didn’t find what was in between quite good enough to ignore that. <div id="review_2327378612" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2327378612" />
    <a title="Jason" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5278185-jason"><img alt="Jason" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1553537834p2/5278185.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2327378612?book_show_action=true">Mar 14, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jason" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jason" href="/user/show/5278185-jason">Jason</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780676972658" href="/book/show/641880.Autobiography_of_Red">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5278185-jason?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5278185-jason?shelf=lgbtqia">lgbtqia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5278185-jason?shelf=canadian">canadian</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2327378612" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8191502246089336524">Upon rereading, from 4 stars to 5. I was either a bad reader when I first read this or just not in the right place. In any case, Carson is a master and I don't think I could live without her now.<br/><br/>“Then he met Herakles and the kingdoms of his life all shifted down a few notches. / They were two superior eels / at the bottom of the tank and they recognized each other like italics.”</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_3002337" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3002337" />
    <a title="Andrew Tibbetts" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/187724-andrew-tibbetts"><img alt="Andrew Tibbetts" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1225662791p2/187724.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3002337?book_show_action=true">Jul 12, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Andrew Tibbetts" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Andrew Tibbetts" href="/user/show/187724-andrew-tibbetts">Andrew Tibbetts</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Any fan of creative literature</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/187724-andrew-tibbetts?shelf=canadian">canadian</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3002337" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15355470565192459769">This novel was written for me, it feels. It has the perfect blend of funny and sad, raw and elegant, intellectual and sensual. It blew my mind when I read it. And it's the one of only two books I've re-read several times (Great Expectations being the other.)<br/><br/>There are some clever metafictional framing sections which come at the material from historical and literary angles, but the central section, the heart of the book, is the story, the novel in verse. <br/><br/>"Verse" in Anne Carson isn't strident metri</span>
  <span id="freeText15355470565192459769" style="display:none">This novel was written for me, it feels. It has the perfect blend of funny and sad, raw and elegant, intellectual and sensual. It blew my mind when I read it. And it's the one of only two books I've re-read several times (Great Expectations being the other.)<br /><br />There are some clever metafictional framing sections which come at the material from historical and literary angles, but the central section, the heart of the book, is the story, the novel in verse. <br /><br />"Verse" in Anne Carson isn't strident metrical regularity or forced rhyme, but merely prose that fine tuned and broken into lines. It's stunningly beautiful. Her highly original way with words is bracing. <br /><br />The story is a retelling of the myth of Hercules and Geryon, the red monster who he kills in one of his labours, as a modern gay romance. Geryon is still a red monster but he's also a sensitive young man who falls in love with impulsive, heroic Hercules, a kind of trailor trash revolutionary. <br /><br />The book has the melancholy, romantic voluptuousness of Thomas Mann or Carson McCullers pressed through the distilling crackle of T.S. Eliot's literate virtuosity. It manages to be Apollonian and Dionysian. Playful, powerful. Hard, soft. Hopeless, hopeful. There isn't a more successful fusion of opposites in literature.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15355470565192459769" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer8575405435655651104">Sometimes I read a work that is so complete that I don't want to write a review. <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/61049.Autobiography_of_Red' title='Autobiography of Red by Anne Carson' rel='nofollow noopener'>Autobiography of Red</a> by <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/34336.Anne_Carson' title='Anne Carson' rel='nofollow noopener'>Anne Carson</a> is that kind of book.<br/><br/>A retelling of the story of Geryon, a red-winged monster who has a short but painful affair with Heracles that reverberates through his life.<br/><br/>What struck me was Geryon's unending effort to make art out of his life-first through writing, even as a child before he could actually write, and then as a photographer. Geryon's life is painful but he constantly searche</span>
  <span id="freeText8575405435655651104" style="display:none">Sometimes I read a work that is so complete that I don't want to write a review. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/61049.Autobiography_of_Red" title="Autobiography of Red by Anne Carson" rel="nofollow noopener">Autobiography of Red</a> by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/34336.Anne_Carson" title="Anne Carson" rel="nofollow noopener">Anne Carson</a> is that kind of book.<br /><br />A retelling of the story of Geryon, a red-winged monster who has a short but painful affair with Heracles that reverberates through his life.<br /><br />What struck me was Geryon's unending effort to make art out of his life-first through writing, even as a child before he could actually write, and then as a photographer. Geryon's life is painful but he constantly searches to give it meaning, to find meaning in the world around him.<br /><br />The poetry is powerful and the story disturbing. I loved this text but I think I would have to read it several more times before I could begin to write a real review.<br /><br />It's a volume well worth re-reading. </span>

<div id="review_13867764" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/13867764" />
    <a title="Pewterbreath" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/657098-pewterbreath"><img alt="Pewterbreath" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1383184514p2/657098.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/13867764?book_show_action=true">Jan 28, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Pewterbreath" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Pewterbreath" href="/user/show/657098-pewterbreath">Pewterbreath</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer13867764" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer789008272818875905">Not in a long time have I obsessively read anything, just to want to obsessively re-read it all in one fell swoop. There's two parts to this book: first is the meta-writing bit--poem fragments and the like from the original Hercules myth, a writer who goes blind for insulting Helen, and then regains his sight again for rescinding his comment (how very political) and the like. This part is good (not great--just good).<br/><br/>It's the story itself that is absolutely wonderful. Anne Carson is good at many </span>
  <span id="freeText789008272818875905" style="display:none">Not in a long time have I obsessively read anything, just to want to obsessively re-read it all in one fell swoop. There's two parts to this book: first is the meta-writing bit--poem fragments and the like from the original Hercules myth, a writer who goes blind for insulting Helen, and then regains his sight again for rescinding his comment (how very political) and the like. This part is good (not great--just good).<br /><br />It's the story itself that is absolutely wonderful. Anne Carson is good at many things: making a good turn of phrase (someone "falls out of the room like an olive off a plate"), good at weaving themes in and out of a narrative (her theme is good too: stories erode. That's it, but as simple as the theme can be stated, it leads to fascinating conclusions), but first and foremost she is exceptionally talented at understanding people. This is may be a pomo pseudo-pseudo myth, but the moments described are so intimate and private she manages to escape the depersonalization that many myths entail.<br /><br />She has given long narrative verse a shot in the arm, that's for sure--I can't think of many instances where an author has so willfully chosen a "difficult" medium and successfully made it so approachable. I couldn't recommend this more.</span>
  <a data-text-id="789008272818875905" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer508243584" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer7795115047627271169">This was surprisingly engaging, emotional, and compulsively plot-driven. I've found other Carson texts occasionally too knotty and the first 20 pages made it seem like this was more of the same. But the importance of the set-up soon becomes clear and gives way to a poetically charged and compelling coming-of-age story that seamlessly mixes modern life with Greek legend. There are lots of wonderful reviews for this, so the only thing I'll add is that if you've been hesitating DON'T BE DAUNTED. Ta</span>
  <span id="freeText7795115047627271169" style="display:none">This was surprisingly engaging, emotional, and compulsively plot-driven. I've found other Carson texts occasionally too knotty and the first 20 pages made it seem like this was more of the same. But the importance of the set-up soon becomes clear and gives way to a poetically charged and compelling coming-of-age story that seamlessly mixes modern life with Greek legend. There are lots of wonderful reviews for this, so the only thing I'll add is that if you've been hesitating DON'T BE DAUNTED. Take the first 20 pages on faith and you'll be rewarded immediately thereafter. </span>

<div id="review_3046100117" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3046100117" />
    <a title="Eric Anderson" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/28819314-eric-anderson"><img alt="Eric Anderson" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1392748007p2/28819314.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3046100117?book_show_action=true">Nov 12, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Eric Anderson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Eric Anderson" href="/user/show/28819314-eric-anderson">Eric Anderson</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780224059732" href="/book/show/1554721.Autobiography_of_Red">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3046100117" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10116523962166439235">I’ve been meaning to begin reading Anne Carson’s poetry for some time. Multiple people recommended I start with “Autobiography of Red” and I’m so glad I read this incredibly inventive book! Carson translated the existing fragments of a poem about a red winged monster with a human face named Geryon which was written by the Greek lyric poet Stesichorus’ who lived from 630-555 BC. In the opening sections Carson imaginatively discusses Stesichorus’ life and the legend of his being rendered blind for</span>
  <span id="freeText10116523962166439235" style="display:none">I’ve been meaning to begin reading Anne Carson’s poetry for some time. Multiple people recommended I start with “Autobiography of Red” and I’m so glad I read this incredibly inventive book! Carson translated the existing fragments of a poem about a red winged monster with a human face named Geryon which was written by the Greek lyric poet Stesichorus’ who lived from 630-555 BC. In the opening sections Carson imaginatively discusses Stesichorus’ life and the legend of his being rendered blind for insulting Helen of Troy. Carson has said she found it difficult to satisfactorily translate his writing successfully so she’s taken the liberty of imaginatively filling out the story of Geryon’s life in this epic narrative poem which reads more like a novella. It takes elements from Geryon’s story but inserts them into a blend of the modern and mythic following his relationship with his mother, sexual abuse by his brother and his romantic entanglements with a man named Herakles (in the myth Heracles kills Geryon by shooting him with an arrow.) It’s stunning how she captures the sensations of Geryon’s life and his unique perspective: his feelings of alienation, artistic aspirations and sexual yearnings. And it’s so beautifully written with many complex lines and metaphors that made me pause and think. <br /><br />Read my full <a href="http://lonesomereader.com/blog/2019/11/12/autobiography-of-red-by-anne-carson" rel="nofollow noopener">review of Autobiography of Red by Anne Carson on LonesomeReader</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="10116523962166439235" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_798704507" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/798704507" />
    <a title="mwpm" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13827810-mwpm"><img alt="mwpm" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1516943741p2/13827810.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/798704507?book_show_action=true">Dec 24, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="mwpm" class="user" itemprop="url" name="mwpm" href="/user/show/13827810-mwpm">mwpm</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13827810-mwpm?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13827810-mwpm?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer798704507" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6722447264096797084">I'm reminded of Michael Ondaatje's book of poetry, <i>
  <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/5947.The_Collected_Works_of_Billy_the_Kid' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Collected Works of Billy the Kid</a>
</i>. The book is a "verse novel" that loosely follows the exploits of Billy the Kid under the pretext that he is the author (though Ondaatje frequently deviates). There are aspects of this in <i>Autobiography of Red</i> (as you may have gathered from the title).<br/><br/>The idea, a classic story from another perspective, is more reminiscent of John Gardner's Grendel (Beowulf from the perspective of the monster). The same app</span>
  <span id="freeText6722447264096797084" style="display:none">I'm reminded of Michael Ondaatje's book of poetry, <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/5947.The_Collected_Works_of_Billy_the_Kid" rel="nofollow noopener">The Collected Works of Billy the Kid</a>
</i>. The book is a "verse novel" that loosely follows the exploits of Billy the Kid under the pretext that he is the author (though Ondaatje frequently deviates). There are aspects of this in <i>Autobiography of Red</i> (as you may have gathered from the title).<br /><br />The idea, a classic story from another perspective, is more reminiscent of John Gardner's Grendel (Beowulf from the perspective of the monster). The same approach has been employed in various modern novels, such as Gregory Maguire's <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/37442.Wicked" rel="nofollow noopener">Wicked</a>
</i> (Baum's Oz series from the perspective of the Wicked Witch).<br /><br />It is employed here to uncanny effect. I say "uncanny" because the author retains a classical form (like <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/5947.The_Collected_Works_of_Billy_the_Kid" rel="nofollow noopener">Billy the Kid</a>
</i> it is "a novel in verse") but sets the action in the twentieth century (unspecified). Something else I liked about the form, the verse portion of the novel (that is the "Autobiography of Red") is established with a fake introduction and appendix. This variation of the epistolary novel is reminiscent of Nabokov's <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7805.Pale_Fire" rel="nofollow noopener">Pale Fire</a>
</i>. But, whereas the poem "Pale Fire" takes up a small portion of the Nabokov's novel and the rest is preoccupied with academic scrutiny, the scrutiny of the "Autobiography of Red" is a small portion of Carson's novel and the rest is dedicated to the so-called text. <br /><br />The author deviates from the original story in various ways. The Greek Myth, from which the characters have been extracted, is summarized in the introduction. I didn't know the myth but this didn't detract from my reading. Knowing the basics of the myth from the summary made me aware of the author's decision to deviate. For example, the relationship of the central characters in the myth is drastically altered. They do not follow the same course of action. They do not belong to the same world, but they have brought aspects of the mythical world into our world. The monster (Geryon, aka Red) retains his wings. His presence - a red monster in a world of telephones, cigarettes, refrigerators - should be absurd, but is often overlooked. In fact, the other characters don't seem to notice (at least they don't acknowledge). For this reason, the character swings between feeling invisible and feeling like a sore thumb.<br /><br />This is a curious text. I'm sure it will appeal to those who enjoy formal experimentation (along the lines of <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7805.Pale_Fire" rel="nofollow noopener">Pale Fire</a>
</i>, <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/24800.House_of_Leaves" rel="nofollow noopener">House of Leaves</a>
</i>, <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/630604.L_Abb_C" rel="nofollow noopener">L'Abbe C</a>
</i>). It may also appeal to those who are interested in classical mythology, taking into consideration that the story is a departure.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6722447264096797084" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_129363035" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/129363035" />
    <a title="Kathryn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1497350-kathryn"><img alt="Kathryn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1250569584p2/1497350.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/129363035?book_show_action=true">Nov 04, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kathryn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kathryn" href="/user/show/1497350-kathryn">Kathryn</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1497350-kathryn?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1497350-kathryn?shelf=religion-myth">religion-myth</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer129363035" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4108001424701132173">I liked this very much. The entire work is perfection, with the exception of the evasive ending. The awe I felt while reading was lessened due to this, which makes me very sad as this is a beautiful and heartwrenching book. I really wish it had ended with a stronger conclusion. <br/><br/>This book managed to smack against my ick wall pretty early. Umm, yeah, I am so not qualified to write a review or anything resembling a review about this book. <br/><br/>I found the book accessible yet distant, dreamy but familiar</span>
  <span id="freeText4108001424701132173" style="display:none">I liked this very much. The entire work is perfection, with the exception of the evasive ending. The awe I felt while reading was lessened due to this, which makes me very sad as this is a beautiful and heartwrenching book. I really wish it had ended with a stronger conclusion. <br /><br />This book managed to smack against my ick wall pretty early. Umm, yeah, I am so not qualified to write a review or anything resembling a review about this book. <br /><br />I found the book accessible yet distant, dreamy but familiar. Carson wrote in a tone I am unused to but learned to love quickly. The author crawled into her character's head and grasped so much, understood so much of what it is to be young, in lust, unsure, and in pain. Geryon is one of the most fascinating characters I have ever read. He saw the world unlike anyone else in the book. For example,<br /><br /><i>It was the year he began to wonder about the noise that colors make. Roses came<br />roaring across the garden at him. <br />He lay on his bed at night listening to the silver light of stars crashing against<br />the window screen...<br />Why is grass called blades? he asked them. Isn't it because of the clicking?</i> <br /><br />Geryon is a romantic, a red monster with wings, lost in the world. I knew before starting that in Greek myth, Geryon was killed by Heracles, a hindrance to the hero completing his tenth labour. Carson included commentary and analysis, at times wry and loosely interpreted, of Stesichorus' incomplete poem about Geryon. Once Carson began her novel in verse, the reader was prepared for a young, humanized character, which Carson was quick to provide a back story for, eliciting deep sympathy from the reader. <br /><br />This is one of those books I want to buy to have on my shelves so I can periodically take it down and again read one of the many beautiful or startling lines Carson wrote. I hope to read more of her work soon. </span>
  <a data-text-id="4108001424701132173" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer133782467" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15929689161393224428">Formally very beautiful, delivers all the pleasures (celerity, compression, wit) I associate with "A Novel in Verse." But I found the story (assuming it can be considered apart from form) wan and Geryon vaguely annoying. Looking forward to <i>Plainwater</i>. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_816295046" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/816295046" />
    <a title="Brenda Cregor" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2497735-brenda-cregor"><img alt="Brenda Cregor" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1526603915p2/2497735.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/816295046?book_show_action=true">Jan 07, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brenda Cregor" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brenda Cregor" href="/user/show/2497735-brenda-cregor">Brenda Cregor</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer816295046" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13945096705360323704">This will only make some, albeit sarcastic, sense, if you have actually read the book:<br/><br/>CLEARING UP<br/>THE QUESTION OF<br/>WHETHER <i>The Autobiography of Red</i><br/>IS WORTH THE PAPER IT<br/>IS PRINTED ON<br/><br/>1. Either THE AUTOBIOGRAPHY OF RED is a good story/poem or it is not.<br/><br/>2. If the book/poem was good, either the goodness of it will enlighten the mind of its reader or it will not.<br/><br/>3. If it is not a book/poem which enlightens the mind of its reader, then there is a reason this is so.<br/><br/>4. If the reason is that the "protagonist</span>
  <span id="freeText13945096705360323704" style="display:none">This will only make some, albeit sarcastic, sense, if you have actually read the book:<br /><br />CLEARING UP<br />THE QUESTION OF<br />WHETHER <i>The Autobiography of Red</i><br />IS WORTH THE PAPER IT<br />IS PRINTED ON<br /><br />1. Either THE AUTOBIOGRAPHY OF RED is a good story/poem or it is not.<br /><br />2. If the book/poem was good, either the goodness of it will enlighten the mind of its reader or it will not.<br /><br />3. If it is not a book/poem which enlightens the mind of its reader, then there is a reason this is so.<br /><br />4. If the reason is that the "protagonist"-monster never took his mistakes, and the evils which were acted upon him, which caused him to walk through life unengaged and basically a useless piece of red animated flesh with wings [ think of the STRANGER's protagonist, Meursault...but less of a true monster, since he is not a killer] by finding absolute truth, which could help him triumph over his trials, through application, or he did not.<br /><br />5. If the protagonist never learned to triumph over all which beset him by grasping some absolute eternal truth, his personal anguish was all for nothing, and his mortality was a waste of time.<br /><br />6. If his mortality was a waste of time, then reading about it is a waste of the reader's time.<br /><br />7. If reading about the protagonist's useless life journey was a waste of the reader's time, then this book/poem is not a good read.<br /><br />8. If this book/poem is not a good read, then it should get one-star.<br /><br />9. If this book has only been rated with one star...it should be burned...so at least the pulpy-pages can be used to do SOMETHING positive, such as provide a bit of warmth to the person who purchased them.<br /><br />10. If it should be burned, the reader who rated it with one star should light the match...<br /><br />The BEST part of the book, NOT the story/poem written by Anne Carson, was the bits and pieces of the writing from which she took inspiration written centuries ago by STESICHOROS.<br /><br />The ACTUAL fragments , really several paragraphs only, about STESICHOROS'S protagonist, the monster, Geryon, were the real gems.<br /><br />I was hoping...hoping...Carson's writing would continue in Stesichoros's same vein...NOT.<br /><br />She adulterated it.<br /><br /><br /><br /><br />There is a way of viewing contemporary poetry and novels which has become common in the analysis of those who are well respected as "intellectual" literary critics.<br /> It is not a new or fresh way of seeing writing.<br /> No.<br /> In fact, it is quite in line with the humanistic philosophies which have elevated and simultaneously plagued mankind for centuries.<br /> No absolutes.<br /> Anything goes.<br /> The more base, the more "worthy".<br /> If you want to know what this book is about, it comes to this: a boy is sexually abused by his big brother, his parents are pretty much disconnected from their offspring emotionally, the boy grows into a teen who gets involved with a perverse self-centered volcano-obsessed young guy, they break up, the boy [ oh he thinks he has wings] grows up looking at the world much as some type of savant might [or is that just the author's way of injecting figurative language into the verse?], he drifts, does nothing but take pictures that make sense to him, he gets back with the vapid disgusting young man, and then goes to see bread ovens built in the side of a volcano.<br /> All of this poetic dribble is accomplished in 160 pages, double spaced, with tiny font and executed quite "prettily", of course.<br /> After all, the "intellectual" "literaries" of the hour must have textual garbage validated by incredibly GORGEOUS language [with the exception of some foul language and graphic scenes, which pop up about five or six times in the book---more than needed, but apparently deemed necessary by the authoress to tell this useless tale].<br /> I felt nothing but a gnawing disgust after I finished the last page.<br /><br /> "HONEY, get the fire pit ready!"</span>
  <a data-text-id="13945096705360323704" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_23049638" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/23049638" />
    <a title="Ruby" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/719205-ruby"><img alt="Ruby" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1319960536p2/719205.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/23049638?book_show_action=true">May 27, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ruby" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ruby" href="/user/show/719205-ruby">Ruby</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">poets, underdogs, people who have fallen in love with a breeze</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/719205-ruby?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/719205-ruby?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/719205-ruby?shelf=teen-obsessions">teen-obsessions</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/719205-ruby?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer23049638" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer64803292479631466">This book marks, without an ember of doubt, the first time I've ever felt burned by my lack of education in the classics. I approached this book ready to feel cowed and lost, so I was enthralled when that was not the case.<br/><br/>I understand Geryon intimately, for I, too am a red creature.<br/><br/>From a forgotten notebook of mine:<br/><br/> "On my steady diet of nicotine and coffee, my thoughts grind (like bad teeth) into points. I am a sharp-shaped thing. A needle, an arrow, I cut. I can touch rage: rage that was the o</span>
  <span id="freeText64803292479631466" style="display:none">This book marks, without an ember of doubt, the first time I've ever felt burned by my lack of education in the classics. I approached this book ready to feel cowed and lost, so I was enthralled when that was not the case.<br /><br />I understand Geryon intimately, for I, too am a red creature.<br /><br />From a forgotten notebook of mine:<br /><br /> "On my steady diet of nicotine and coffee, my thoughts grind (like bad teeth) into points. I am a sharp-shaped thing. A needle, an arrow, I cut. I can touch rage: rage that was the only sprig of life on the barren potato farm; rage tucked into the left work boot for the dark walk home from the plant; rage channeled into the line of a razor's making, at first invisible, then blessed red. We all know the color of rage. Red will unmake me."<br /><br />Geryon's red is a different hue, as has my own ripened with age. Passion. Shame. Love. The interior exposed and vulnerable. Heat. Longing. Did you know longing was red? Do you know how close you are to knowing that?<br /><br /> Like the terrestrial crust of the earth<br /> which is proportionately ten times thinner than an eggshell, the skin of the soul<br /> is a miracle of mutual pressures.<br /><br />Fuck Herakles. That bitch and his arrogance, never seeing the deep red interior of his jailbait trick. Winning is blindness. Winning is empty. Winning is lonely, even with a joint in one hand and a cock in the other. It is through losing that we learn to make bread in the volcano's eye. It is through returning that we get wings.<br /><br />Anne Carson, thank you for making a hero of the vanquished, for turning a flat story over and finding the life growing beneath it.<br /><br /> Geryon stood upright<br /> within the rayon planes of his brother's sports jacket. Sweat and desire ran<br /> down his body to pool<br /> in the crotch and behind the knees. He had been standing against the wall<br /> for three and a half hours in a casual pose.<br /> His eyes ached from the effort of trying to see everything without looking at it.<br /> Other boys stood beside him<br /> on the wall. The petals of their colognes rose about them in a light terror.<br /> Meanwhile music pounded<br /> across hearts opening every valve to the desperate drama of being<br /> a self in a song.<br /><br />"What is time made of?" Geryon asks frequently.<br /><br /> Fear of time came at him. Time<br /> was squeezing Geryon like the pleats of an accordion.<br /><br />And:<br /><br /> ...A man moves through time. It means nothing except that,<br /> like a harpoon, once thrown he will arrive.<br /><br />What does this thoughtful young artist have against time? We might think it's his death -- we all know his demise is assured before reading the book, or at least once we find out he goes up against Herakles:<br /><br /> on the other side of the world somewhere Herakles laughing drinking getting<br /> into a car and Geryon's<br /> whole body formed one arch of a cry -- upcast to that custom, the human custom of wrong love.<br /><br />But here Carson has turned the story around -- it's not death Geryon waits for, but heartbreak. And heartbreak, as we all can't help but know, is red like thunder.</span>
  <a data-text-id="64803292479631466" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
